рд╕рд╛рдпрдг-рднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрдореН

тАШ рдкреНрд░рд╛рддрд╛ рд░рддреНрдирдореН ’ рдЗрддрд┐ рд╕рдкреНрддрд░реНрдЪрдВ рдкрдЮреНрдЪрдордВ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрдВ рдХрд╛рдХреНрд╖реАрд╡рддрдореН ред рджрд╛рдирд╕реНрдп рд╕реНрддреВрдпрдорд╛рдирддреНрд╡рд╛рддреН рджрд╛рдирджреЗрд╡рддреНрдпрдВ ’ рдпрд╛ рддреЗрдиреЛрдЪреНрдпрддреЗ рд╕рд╛ рджреЗрд╡рддрд╛ ’ ( рдЕрдиреБ. реи. рел ) рдЗрддрд┐ рдкрд░рд┐рднрд╛рд╖рд┐рддрддреНрд╡рд╛рддреН ред рддреНрд░реИрд╖реНрдЯреБрднрдореН ред рдЙрдкреЗрддреНрдпрд╛рджрд┐рдХреЗ рджреНрд╡реЗ рдЬрдЧрддреНрдпреМ ред тАШ рдкреНрд░рд╛рддрд╛ рд░рддреНрдирдВ рд╕рдкреНрдд рд╕реНрд╡рдирдпрд╕реНрдп рджрд╛рдирд╕реНрддреБрддрд┐рд░реБрдкрдЬрдЧрддреНрдпреМ ’ рдЗрддреНрдпрдиреБрдХреНрд░рдордгрд┐рдХрд╛ рее

Jamison Brereton

125
SvanayaтАЩs Da╠Дnastuti
Kakс╣г─лvant Dairghatamasa
7 verses: triс╣гс╣нubh, except jagat─л 4тАУ5
This hymn falls into two distinct but thematically connected parts. The second part (vss. 4тАУ7) develops an extravagant picture of the rewards that accrue to the gener ous giver and sacrificer. The whole cosmos seems to exist only to gratify him, and a place among the gods as an immortal awaits him. The syntax and sentiments are straightforward and indeed somewhat simplistic.
The other section (vss. 1тАУ3) is quite different. It consists of an apparent dia logue between an тАЬearly-comingтАЭ figure (vs. 3) and, probably, a householder or host (vs. 2), with a scene-setting introduction (vs. 1). The word тАЬearly-comingтАЭ (pr─Бtar├нtvan) is found only in this hymn and in a narrative in the K─Бс╣нhaka Saс╣Гhit─Б (besides the later etymological work, the Nirukta), where the same dialogic rela tionship between the Pr─Бtaritvan and a householder, in that case Manu, obtains. In both passages the Pr─Бtaritvan seems to be an itinerant priest or poet, offer ing his ritual services to a sacrificer, his potential host. But he also stands in for or represents a god who is coming to attend the early-morning ritual. (Compare the compound of similar form and similar meaning pr─Бtary─Б╠Бvan тАЬearly-driving, early-traveling,тАЭ used especially of the A┼Ыvins and their chariot.) Thus, the host is

more inclined to receive the itinerant Pr─Бtaritvan because he may be entertaining a god in disguise, and the Pr─Бtaritvan is not only seeking payment for his ritual work, the dakс╣гiс╣З─Б whose giver is lyrically celebrated in verses 4тАУ7, but also himself brings (or claims to bring) goods and good fortune for his host, as is emphasized in all three verses of this portion of the hymn. The relationship between them is also presented as somewhat coercive or threatening in the second half of verse 2 (which unfortunately contains several hapaxes and is therefore not very clear). The K─Бс╣нhaka Saс╣Гhit─Б narrative, a version of тАЬManuтАЩs Cups,тАЭ depicts that threat much more strongly, as an aspect of the тАЬanxieties of hospitality.тАЭ For further discussion see Jamison (1996a: 184тАУ89).

Jamison Brereton Notes

(The Early-coming one.) Svayana’s D─Бnastuti For a sustained treatment of the тАЬearly-coming oneтАЭ (pr─Бt├бritvan-) see Jamison, Sac.Wife 184-89.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬHaving come in the early morning, (Svanaya) presents precious (wealth), knowing it (to be worthy of acceptance); and having, (therefore), accepted it, (Kakс╣г─лvat) brings it (to his father); wherewith the parent of excellent sons, maintaining his progeny, passes his life in the enjoyment of affluence.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Legend: Kakс╣г─лvat, having finished his course of study, and taken leave of his preceptor, journeyed homewards. As he fell asleep by the road-side, R─Бj─Б Svanaya, the son of Bh─Бvavyavya, attended by his retinue, came by and disturbed the br─Бhmaс╣Зa’s slumber. R─Бj─Б was struck by Kakс╣г─лvat’s person nality and got the latter married to ten of his daughters, presenting him with a hundred niс╣гkas of gold, a hundred horses, a hundred bulls, one thousand and sixty cows and eleven four-horse-drawn-chariots (one each the wives and one for Kakс╣г─лvat). Kaks─лvat returned home and presented the gifts to his father, D─лrghatamas. The hymn is a praise of the munificence of Svanaya

Jamison Brereton

In the early morning, the early-coming (priest/god) establishes a treasure. An observant man, receiving him (as guest), lays it in himself.
Increasing his own progeny and lifetime with it, possessing good heroes, he is accompanied by thriving of wealth.

Griffith

COMING at early morn he gives his treasure; the prudent one receives and entertains him.
Thereby increasing still his life and offspring, he comes with brave sons to abundant riches.

Geldner

Der Morgengast bringt am Morgen ein Kleinod. Der Verst├дndige, der ihn aufnimmt, legt sich einen Schatz zu. Er mehrt durch ihn seine Nachkommenschaft und seine Lebenszeit; reich an S├╢hnen wird er wachsenden Besitzes teilhaftig.

Grassmann

Der fr├╝hkommende [Opferherr] gibt fr├╝h am Morgen Geschenke, ihn wahrnehmend nimmt sie [der S├дnger] in Empfang und verwahrt sie; dadurch mehrt er seinen Stamm und sein Leben, und steht kr├дftig in der Bl├╝te des Wohlstandes.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬMay he (the R─Бj─Б) be rich in kine, in gold, in horses; may Indra grant abundant food to him who delays you, returning home in the early morning, by costly gifts, as (a hunter arrests the) wandering (animals) by his snares.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

[The host:] тАЬHe will have good cows, good gold, good horsesтАФfor him Indra establishes lofty vigorтАФ
he who, o early-comer, binds you up like a pard [?] with a thong [?], when you come hither with goods.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton Notes

The first three p─Бdas of this verse are in the standard high Rigvedic register and present the unsurprising themes of prosperity and reciprocity, but the fourth p─Бda violently wrenches the verse in an unexpected direction. The placidly happy relationship between host and guest depicted in vss. 1 and 2abc takes a sinister and coercive turn, but what precisely that turn consists of is partly obscured by the fact that p─Бda d contains two hapaxes (mukс╣г─л╠Бj─Б- and p├бdi-) and a very rare lexeme (├║d тИЪs─Б/si, otherwise only AV VI.112.2-3). What is clear is that the host derives great benefit from the visit of the early-coming one if he forces him to stay – тАЬties him upтАЭ in fact.

A simile adds precision to this picture, or it would if we understood it: mukс╣г─л╠Бjayeva p├бdim тАЬ(binds you up) like a p├бdi with a mukс╣г─л╠Бj─Б-.тАЭ Geldner tr. тАЬwie den Vogel in der Schlinge,тАЭ which certainly yields sense though it is essentially contextually inspired guesswork. (In content it is reminiscent of the clearer III.45.1 тАЬLet no one hold you down, as men using snares do a bird.тАЭ) I have tried pushing it further, though with no confidence in the correctness of my speculations.

I treated mukс╣г─л╠Бj─Б- elsewhere (Ged. Cowgill, 1987, pp. 89-91). I suggested there that the word is a deformation of muс╣гk├б- тАШtesticleтАЩ and that Kakс╣г─лvant is making a play on his own name (which may itself mean тАШhaving a crotchтАЩ), esp. the -kс╣г─л, with this deformation – as he does elsewhere in his oeuvre. Taking this further, the -j─Бmay be the root noun to тИЪjani , a root noun very common as a 2nd compound member (see the numerous exx. in Scarlatta. 132-53). Those compounds are invariably accented on the -j─Б╠Б-; I would attribute the accent here to the poetтАЩs play on his name kakс╣г─л╠Бvant-.

But what would this baroque confection mukс╣г─л╠Бj─Б- mean? If it literally means тАШoriginating from/at the testiclesтАЩ, it could refer to a loin cloth, a strip of cloth that covers the genitals – a thong – and as a long strip of material it could be used to bind or tie up an animal.

My interpr. of p├бdi- rests on even less evidence. I suggest, very tentatively, that it comes from a MIA form *pс╣Ыdi-, related to the Iranian forms borrowed into Greek as ╧А╬м╧Б╬┤╬▒╬╗╬╣╧В, etc., as well as to Skt. pс╣Ыd─Бku-. In earlier lit. this word was said to mean тАШleopard, panther, tigerтАЩ only in lexical texts, while its earlier occurrences mean тАШspotted snakeтАЩ; see EWA, KEWA s.v. Mayrhofer attributes the later lexical meaning to borrowing from Iranian. But it clearly refers to a large wild feline in AVP II.18.1, since it is parallel to siс╣Гha-тАШlionтАЩ and vy─Бghra- тАШtigerтАЩ; see Zehnder, Atharvaveda-Paippal─Бda, Buch 2, p. 59. In keeping with the racy tone in these two hymns, it could also be a pun on тИЪpr╠еd ‘fart’ (though this root is not attested in Vedic [see EWA s.v. pard], its representation in the younger language and in Iranian, incl.

Avestan, suggests that it was known to Vedic speakers), and the desire to make the pun would have led K. to use an otherwise obscure word for wild animal here. Given the discrepancy in morphology (no forms of the shape *pс╣Ыdi- are attested in any relevant language) and the chronological gap, this gossamer hypothesis probably should be discarded – but there is nothing stronger to take its place (and it gave me the opportunity to use the English word тАШpardтАЩ).

Griffith

Rich shall he be in gold and kine and horses. Indra bestows on him great vital power,
Who stays thee, as thou comest, with his treasure, like game caught in the net, O early comer.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬDesirous (of again beholding you), I have this day obtained you, who have done in the morning a good deed; the performer of a sacrifice with a wealth-laden car, refresh yoursel fwiththe effused libation of the exhilarating (Soma), and augment with sincere prayers (the prosperity) of the chief of the flourishing race.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Iс╣гс╣нeс╕е putram = lit. the son of sacrifice;

Putram = purutrataram, the much-protecting; or, kart─Бram, performer

Jamison Brereton

[The early-comer:] тАЬI have come here today early in the morning with a goods-filled chariot, seeking one who performs (sacrifice) well, the son of my seeking.
Make (Indra?) drink the pressed (soma) of the exhilarating plant;
strengthen the hero-ruling (Indra?) with liberal gifts.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton Notes

iс╣гс╣н├йс╕е putr├бm тАЬthe son of my seekingтАЭ picks up the immediately preceding part. ich├бn тАШseekingтАЩ to the same root and means essentially the product of my successful search.

Indra is the likely recipient of the soma in c and the strengthening in d, but the epithet kс╣гay├бdv─лra- (8x) is never applied to Indra, rather usually to Rudra (5x). But Rudra is highly unlikely to be the target here.

Griffith

Longing, I came this morning to the pious, the son of sacrifice, with car wealth-laden.
Give him to drink juice of the stalk that gladdens; prosper with pleasant hymns the Lord of Heroes.

Geldner

Der Morgengast: “Ich kam heute in der Fr├╝he mit sch├дtzereichem Wagen, den Frommen suchend, den Sohn meines Wunsches. Gib den Saft des berauschenden Stengels zu trinken, erbaue den M├дnnerbeherrscher durch Schenkungen!

Grassmann

Heute morgen kam dieser [Opferherr] begehrend zu dem Frommen, dem Sohn des Gebetes, mit Gut-beladnem Wagen; lass ihn trinken den Saft des berauschenden Soma, den M├дnnerherrscher erfreue durch Lobges├дnge.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThe copiously-yielding and joy-conferringkine, distil (their milk) for the celebration of the (Soma) sacrifice; and for him who has undertaken its celebration; the nutritious streams of butter coverage from every quarter towards him, who both propitiates (his progenitors), and benefits (mankind).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Pс╣Ыс╣Зantam ca papurim- ca = agent who gives plural asure or who gives satisfaction; pс╣Ыс╣Зantam is applied to the pitс╣Ыs, or progenitors (pitram pс╣Ыс╣Зayantam; papurm is applied to all living beings (pr─Бс╣Зinaс╕е sarvad─Б pс╣Ыс╣Зayantam puruс╣гam)

Jamison Brereton

The rivers, embodiments of joy, milk-cows, flow near to the one who has sacrificed and the one who will sacrifice;
seeking fame, the streams of ghee on every side go near to him who
grants and has granted.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The two conjoined phrases ─лj─Бn├бс╣Г ca yakс╣гy├бm─Бnaс╣Г ca (b) and pс╣Ыс╣З├бntaс╣Г ca p├бpuriс╣Г ca (c) have the same referent, and their syntactic parallelism invites a completely parallel interpretation of their verbal semantics. But the pairs are not morphologically parallel: the first phrase consists of a perfect part. and a future part., the second of a present part. and a reduplicated -i-stem adj. In the published translation I render p├бpuri- as preterital (тАЬwho тАж has grantedтАЭ), but following Grestenberger (JAOS 113.2 [2013]) I now see such forms as imperfectives, often with habitual or iterative sense; unfortunately in this context the conjoined phrase then seems almost pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++, though perhaps тАЬthe one who grants and keeps grantingтАЭ would work.

I would also slightly alter the tr. of the phrase in b to тАЬthe one who has sacrificed and will sacrifice,тАЭ to make the parallelism of the two phrases in bc clearer and also to rule out a reading in which the two participles in b have different referents.

Keith

Streams, health-bringing, like milch cows,
Pour up to the man who hath sacrificed and shall sacrifice; Him who filleth and satiateth [4], bringing fame,
Streams of ghee approach on all sides.

Griffith

Health-bringing streams, as milch-cows, flow to profit him who hath worshipped, him who now will worship.
To him who freely gives and fills on all sides full streams of fatness flow and make him famous.

Geldner

Erquickend str├╢men dem die Fl├╝sse, dem die Milchk├╝he, der geopfert hat und opfern wird. Dem der reichlich spendet und schenkt, flie├Яen von allen Seiten die Schmalzstr├╢me wetteifernd zu.

Grassmann

Es rinnen lustbringende Str├╢me, die Milchtr├дnke, hin zu dem, der geopfert hat, und zu dem, der opfern will; zu dem, welcher spendet und reichlich schenkt, gehen hohes Gl├╝ck verleihend von allen Seiten die Str├╢me des Fettes.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬHe who propitiates (the gods), gives to the gods, and sits at ease upon the summit of heaven; to him the flowing waters bear their essence; to him this fertile (earth) ever yields abundance.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Iyam dakс╣гiс╣З─Б: by this, capable earth; bh┼лmi, earth, that is able, dakс╣гa, to bear crops

Jamison Brereton

On the back of the firmament he stands firmly fixed. Whoever grants, he goes among the gods;
to him the waters, the rivers stream ghee; for him this gift-cow (dakс╣гiс╣З─Б) swells always.

Griffith

On the high ridge of heaven he stands exalted, yea, to the Gods he goes, the liberal giver.
The streams, the waters flow for him with fatness: to him this guerdon ever yields abundance.

Geldner

Auf die H├╢he des Himmels versetzt bleibt er da. Wer spendet, der kommt zu den G├╢ttern. Ihm str├╢men die Gew├дsser, die Fl├╝sse Schmalz. Ihm wird dieser S├дngerlohn immerdar zur milchstrotzenden Kuh.

Grassmann

Auf dem Gipfel des Himmels steht er erh├╢ht; wer [Opfer] lohnt, der, ja der gelangt zu den G├╢ttern; ihm str├╢men Wasser und Fl├╝sse Fett zu, ihm strotzt von Milch stets diese reichlich str├╢mende Milchkuh.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬThese wonderful (rewards) verily are for those who give (pious) donations; for the donors of (pious) gifts the suns shine in heaven; the givers of (pious) donations attain immortality; the givers of (pious) gifts prolong their (worldly) existence.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Im─Бni citr─Б, these wonders of these variegated things,which are person nal decorations– garlands, sandal, jewels, pearls; dakс╣гiс╣З─Бvat─Бm- dakс╣гiс╣З─Бd─Бtс╣Ыс╣З─Бm, of the givers of dak.с╣гiс╣З─Б or donations to brahmins at the end of a sacrifice, or any particular solemnity

Jamison Brereton

For the givers of dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs only there are these brilliant (bounties) here; for the givers of dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs there are suns in heaven.
Givers of dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs have a share in immortality; givers of dakс╣гiс╣З─Бs
lengthen their own lifetime.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The dakс╣гiс╣З─Б (priestly gift, more literally gift-cow) theme comes to the fore here.

I supply тАШbountiesтАЩ (r─Б╠Бdh─Бс╣Гsi) with citr─Б╠БтАШbrightтАЩ on the basis of the cmpd citr├б-r─Бdhas- and the numerous occurrences of the phrase citr├б- r─Б╠Бdhas-.

Griffith

For those who give rich meeds are all these splendours, for those who give rich meeds suns shine in heaven.
The givers of rich meeds are made immortal; the givers of rich fees prolong their lifetime.

Geldner

Nur f├╝r die, die den S├дngerlohn geben, sind diese Sch├╢nheiten auf Erden; f├╝r die, die den S├дngerlohn geben, sind die Sonnen am Himmel. Die den S├дngerlohn geben, genie├Яen die Unsterblichkeit; die den S├дngerlohn geben, verl├дngern ihr Leben.

Grassmann

Denen, welche das Opfer lohnen, ist gl├дnzend diese Welt, ihnen sind gl├дnzend die Sonnen am Himmel, die Opferlohnenden empfangen Unsterblichkeit, die Opferlohnenden verl├дngern ihr Leben.

Elizarenkova

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬMay those who propitiate (the gods), never commit degrading sin; may those who praise the gods and observe holy vows, never experience decay; may some (honourable) individual ever be their defence; and may afflictions fall upon him who does not propitiate (the gods).тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Anyas teс╣г─Бm paridhir astu ka┼Ыcit, may some other one be their surrounding defence; or, kavacasth─Бn─лya, in the plural ce of armour; anyaс╕е ka┼Ыcit = some other {man, or divinity or merit (dharma-vi┼Ыeс╣г─Бt) or Svanaya as the type of patron or protector}

Jamison Brereton

Let those who grant not encounter difficulty or outrage; let the patrons, of good commandment, not grow old;
let there be some other enclosure for them, but to the non-granting one let there come the flames of pain.

Jamison Brereton Notes

I do not entirely understand p─Бda c, which must contrast with d. I assume the referents of t├йс╣г─Бm are the generous patrons of ab, who are distinguished from the ├бpс╣Ыс╣Зantam тАШnon-granterтАЩ of d. This non-granter is to be entirely engulfed (abh├н s├бm тИЪi) by ┼Ы├│k─Бс╕е. The stem ┼Ы├│ka- and the various forms of the root to which it belongs (тИЪ┼Ыuc) otherwise refers to blazing flames in the RV, but in later Skt. it has come to mean тАШpain, afflictionтАЩ. I think that both senses are present here, hence my portmanteau tr. тАЬflames of pain.тАЭ The flames in this p─Бda may help in interpreting the previous one. One of the uses of paridh├н- тАШenclosureтАЩ is for the тАЬenclosing sticksтАЭ placed around the ─Аhavan─лya fire (already X.90.15 and common in ritual lit.). If the non-giver is being surrounded by flames in d, the givers in c deserve a different and benign enclosure (any├бс╕е тАж paridh├нс╕е тАж k├б┼Ы cid), not the paridh├н- that ordinarily surrounds the fire. Its nature remains unspecified: both the initial position of any├бand the final k├б┼Ы cid mark the referent as indefinite.

Griffith

Let not the liberal sink to sin and sorrow, never decay the pious chiefs who worship!
Let every man besides be their protection, and let affliction fall upon the niggard.

Geldner

Die Spender sollen nicht in S├╝nde und Schuld fallen, nicht sollen die pflichtgetreuen Patrone altersschwach werden. Irgend ein anderer soll deren Ablenker sein. Die Schmerzen sollen den Geizigen treffen.

рд╕рд╡рд┐рддрд╛ рдЬреЛрд╢реА тЖР рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рдо) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ
